However, tremendous volume variation and low conductivity limit its widespread applications. Hence, we design and synthesize uniformly distributed honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous carbon nanospheres (HHPCNSs) with ultralarge pore volume (3.258 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) on a large scale through a facile way. The large pore volume provides enough space for loading of P and the expansion of P, and the uniform distribution of the micro–mesopores enables the red P to load uniformly. The resulting HHPCNSs/P composite exhibits extremely high capacity (2463.8 and 2367.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively), splendid rate performance (842.2 and 831.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively) and superior cycling stability (1201.6 and 938.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles for LIBs and 1269.4 and 861.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles for SIBs, respectively). More importantly, when coupled with LiFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, lithium/sodium-ion full batteries display high capacity and superior rate and cycling performances, revealing the practicability of the HHPCNSs/P composite. The exceptional electrochemical performance is caused by the honeycomb-like carbon network with ultralarge pore volume, uniformly distributed hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure, outstanding electronic conductivity, and excellent nanostructural stability, which is much better than currently reported P/C materials for both LIBs and SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D13513%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DBingqiu%2BLiu%252C%2BQi%2BZhang%252C%2BLu%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D11%26contentID%3Dacsnano.9b07428%26title%3DEncapsulating%2BRed%2BPhosphorus%2Bin%2BUltralarge%2BPore%2BVolume%2BHierarchical%2BPorous%2BCarbon%2BNanospheres%2Bfor%2BLithium%252FSodium-Ion%2BHalf%252FFull%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D13523%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Mixtures"> Mixtures </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292416&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanostructures"> Nanostructures </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrasmall+Red+Phosphorus&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrasmall%20red%20phosphorus">ultrasmall red phosphorus</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultralarge+Pore+Volume&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultralarge%20pore%20volume">ultralarge pore volume</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Micro%E2%88%92mesopores&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_micro%E2%88%92mesopores">micro−mesopores</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Capacity+And+Long+Life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20capacity%20and%20long%20life">high capacity and long life</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium%2Fsodium-ion+Half%2Ffull+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lithium%2Fsodium-ion%20half%2Ffull%20batteries">lithium/sodium-ion half/full batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been used in large-scale applications, such as portable electronic devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 1 to 9">(1−9)</a> Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have also attracted much attention because of the abundant sodium resource and their low cost. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 10 to 18">(10−18)</a> Many strategies have been proposed to develop anode materials for high-performance LIBs and SIBs, because the low capacity of commercial graphite cannot meet the increasing requirements. Among various anode materials, red phosphorus (P) is considered to be a promising anode candidate for LIBs and SIBs, due to the low cost and the high theoretical capacity (2596 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a> However, red P suffers from poor electronic conductivity and tremendous volume variation during the discharge/charge process, resulting in poor electronic kinetics, large polarization, and further low active material utilization, as well as drastic capacity fading. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 23 to 28">(23−28)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To solve the above problems, combining P with porous carbon materials has been regarded as the most effective strategy due to the improved conductivity and accommodated volume change. However, simplex pore-type carbon materials cannot satisfy the requirements because of the unsatisfied P loading content and pore blocking caused by red P infiltration, which leads to poor cycle performance and decreased electrode–electrolyte contact area. Hierarchical micro–mesoporous carbon materials have attracted more attention because micropores can not only be beneficial for adsorbing red P nanoparticles through the robust adsorption force but also confine red P to the nanosize. Besides, the mesopores are preferential to increase the contact area of the electrode–electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a> For example, Yang <i>et</i><i>al</i>. encapsulated red P nanoparticles into seaweed-derived hierarchical porous carbonaceous fibers, which obtained 36.7% loading content of red P, leading to the high capacity of 1130 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 370 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Nevertheless, most micropores of hierarchical micro–mesoporous carbon materials are not evenly distributed, resulting in nonuniform size of red P, which easily causes agglomeration during cycling. So developing evenly distributed micropores in the hierarchical micro–mesoporous carbon materials to obtain uniformly ultrasmall red P is an effective method to improve electrochemical property. In addition, the loading content of red P in ordinary hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure is still very low, and although it solves some of the volume expansion problems, most red P with porous carbon materials still face a rapid capacity degradation, leading to a poor cycling performance of less than 100 cycles, which is caused by the huge volume change of red P. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 and 32">(30,32)</a> Therefore, it needs a large space to increase red P loading content and alleviate volume expansion. The hierarchical porous carbon materials with large pore volume emerge as times require, which is expected to increase the loading of red P and accommodate a huge volume change to keep the nanostructure stable. Moreover, the large pore volume also accommodates more electrolyte to enhance the electrode–electrolyte contact area, thus affording sufficient space for electrolyte ions to access red P in the deep pore. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> But, the synthesis of a large pore volume of carbon materials is very difficult, which often use pluronic amphiphilic triblock copolymers, such as P123 and F127, and the chemical activation occurred at high temperature with activating agents, such as KOH. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Although the pore volume can be enlarged by these strategies, most of the porous carbon materials still possess relatively low porosity with a pore volume below 1 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, while a pore volume larger than 2 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> is hard and it is very rarely over 3 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Therefore, it is highly desirable to synthesize uniformly distributed hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure with ultralarge pore volume through a facile method as the host for red P to achieve the above two points, which will allow the P anode to be a choice for LIBs and SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we design and synthesize uniformly distributed honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous carbon nanospheres (HHPCNSs) with an ultralarge pore volume of 3.258 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> by a facile method. Then, the HHPCNSs/P composite is obtained through a vaporization–condensation (V–C) process. The nanostructure possesses an even distribution of micro–mesopores, which makes the red P loading uniform and small. Meanwhile, the large pore volume provides enough space for loading red P and the expansion of red P. When tested as an anode for LIBs and SIBs, the HHPCNSs/P composite exhibits an ultrahigh capacity (1876.1 and 1674.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles for LIBs and SIBs, respectively), outstanding rate performance (842.2 and 831.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively), and superior cycling stability (1201.6 and 938.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retentions of 80.1% and 75.7% after 1000 cycles for LIBs and 1269.4 and 861.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retentions of 81.2% and 70.1% after 1000 cycles for SIBs, respectively). Furthermore, HHPCNSs/P composite//LiFePO<sub>4</sub> and HHPCNSs/P composite//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells display high capacities of 1106.6 and 1017.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and retain as much as 834.2 and 793.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a> displays a schematic illustration of the fabrication processes of the HHPCNSs/P composite. First, Ca(OH)<sub>2</sub> was added to a poly(acrylic acid) (PAA) aqueous solution to form a PAA-Ca aqueous solution. Then, dispersed PAA-Ca NSs were generated by adding isopropyl alcohol (IPA) dropwise to the PAA-Ca solution, revealed by the color changing from transparent to white (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a and b</a>). Subsequently, the product was calcined at 700 °C under an argon atmosphere to afford CaO/C. The HHPCNSs were acquired by removing CaO through acid treatment, resulting in the honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesopore nanostructure. It can be seen that there are three pore sizes, including two mesopores and one micropore. The micropores are formed by pyrolyzing PAA-Ca chains, because the introduction of Ca<sup>2+</sup> into the PAA chains leads to an noncompact carbon layer after pyrolysis, while the etching of CaO from the carbon network and on the intersection of the carbon network results in the mesopores. The details of the three pores will be confirmed in the following sections. Finally, red P was encapsulated into the HHPCNSs through a V–C process. The morphologies and structures are examined by transmission electron (TEM) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) imaging. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the TEM image of uniform and disperse PAA-Ca NSs with a diameter of 150 nm. After calcining at 700 °C, spherical-shaped CaO/C NSs were generated, and the surface of the NSs changed from smooth to rough (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b exhibits a single CaO/C NS, which consists of a number of small CaO subunits in a PAA-derived carbon network. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c displays the typical SEM images of the CaO/C NSs with uniform diameters of 150 nm. After etching by HCl (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d), HHPCNSs are obtained without cracking, and the mesopores are well distributed in the HHPCNSs. The morphology of the HHPCNSs/P composite is well retained through the V–C process, except that the pores are occupied by ultrafine P. The elemental mapping images of a single HHPCNS and HHPCNS/P composite in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e–h and k–n demonstrate that the C (blue), O (orange) and C (blue), P (red) are uniformly distributed in HHPCNS and the HHPCNS/P composite. SEM images of HHPCNSs and the HHPCNSs/P composite are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>i and o, and it can be obviously seen that the HHPCNSs/P composite retains its original morphology with a large amount of pores on the surface after V–C treatment, indicating that red phosphorus is basically loaded into the pores without red P on the surface. In contrast, there is a large amount of P agglomerating in the HHPCNS/P mixture (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), revealing that a number of P fails to be confined in the HHPCNSs network. Furthermore, about 0.5 g of HHPCNSs can be successfully obtained per single synthesis run in this experiment (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a>).</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic illustration of the synthesis process for the HHPCNSs/P composite.</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. TEM images of (a) PAA-Ca NSs, (b) CaO/C NSs, inset: HRTEM image of a single CaO/C NS. (c) SEM images of CaO/C NSs, inset: a single CaO/C NS. TEM images of (d) HHPCNSs and (j) the HHPCNSs/P composite, inset: HRTEM image of a single HHPCNS and HHPCNS/P composite. SEM images of (i) HHPCNSs and (o) the HHPCNSs/P composite, inset: a single HHPCNS and HHPCNS/P composite. The elemental mapping images of (e) a single HHPCNS: (f) mix, (g) C, and (h) O and (k) a single HHPCNS/P composite: (l) mix, (m) C, and (n) P energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) maps.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To study the compositions of the HHPCNSs/P composite, X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectroscopy were examined. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a displays the XRD patterns of red P, HHPCNSs, and the HHPCNSs/P composite. HHPCNSs exhibit two broad peaks at 2θ ≈ 26° and 43°, indicating an amorphous state of carbon. After encapsulating red P into HHPCNSs, the XRD pattern for the HHPCNSs/P composite represents weak peaks of P for the HHPCNSs/P composite without additional peaks, demonstrating red P is ultrasmall and successfully encapsulated into the HHPCNS network. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Similar observations can be seen in the Raman spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). No changes can be seen in the carbon Raman peaks, revealing that the incorporation of red P does not result in structure damage of the HHPCNSs. There are two peaks at 1351 and 1389 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the Raman spectra, corresponding to D (degree of disorder carbon) and G (graphitic order) bands, respectively. The intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is calculated to demonstrate the degree of graphitization in carbons. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> slightly increases form 1.01 for HHPCNSs to 1.04 for the HHPCNSs/P composite, demonstrating that more defective sites were created after encapsulating red P into the HHPCNSs <i>via</i> the V–C strategy. The P content in the HHPCNSs/P composite was examined by thermogravimetry analysis (TGA) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> As expected, the P content in the HHPCNSs/P composite is calculated to be 58.1%. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was further used to detect chemical interactions between red P and HHPCNSs. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c displays the XPS survey spectra of HHPCNSs and the HHPCNSs/P composite. Two peaks can be seen for the HHPCNSs/P composite, corresponding to the characteristic peaks of P 2p and C 1s. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d compares the high-resolution XPS P 2p spectra of red P and the HHPCNSs/P composite. Two peaks at 129.6 and 130.4 eV can be seen in the P–P 2p spectrum of red P, and these P–P bond peaks are shifted in the spectrum of the HHPCNSs/P composite, revealing chemical interactions between red P and HHPCNSs. Moreover, new peaks are centered at 131.2, 132.3, and 134.5 eV, which can be ascribed to P–C and P–O<sub><i>x</i></sub> (P–O–C) bonds, respectively, appearing for the HHPCNSs/P composite due to the V–C process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> By comparison, these peaks disappear in the P 2p spectrum of the HHPCNSs/P mixture (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms were examined to detect pore size distribution, specific surface area, and pore volume of HHPCNSs and the HHPCNSs/P composites. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e displays that HHPCNSs exhibit a typical type IV characteristic with a well-defined plateau in the low-pressure range and a clear hysteresis loop, demonstrating the presence of a significant amount of micropores and mesopores in the HHPCNSs. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f exhibits the pore distributions calculated by density functional theory (DFT). The HHPCNSs display a uniform narrow pore size distribution mainly at 0.9, 3.1, and 8.5 nm. The pore with a size of 0.9 nm is formed by pyrolysis of PAA-Ca chains, while the pore size of 3.1 nm can be attributed to the etching of CaO on the intersection of the carbon network. The etching of CaO from the carbon network results in the pore size of 8.5 nm. Moreover, the HHPCNSs exhibit a high specific surface area of 1013.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and ultralarge pore volume of 3.258 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, consisting of a micropore volume of 0.526 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and mesopore volume of 2.732 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. The high pore volume is caused by the honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure with abundant open mesopores, which can be seen in the SEM images. After combining red P, the micropore of 0.9 nm and mesopore of 3.1 nm disappear and only a small amount of mesopores of 8.5 nm remains. Furthermore, both pore volume and surface area of the HHPCNSs/P composite diminish to 0.508 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 75.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, revealing micropores and most mesopores in HHPCNSs are inhabited by P. Obviously, the pore volume is still left a little to alleviate the huge volume expansion of P during cycling, increase the electrode–electrolyte contact area, and provide sufficient space for electrolyte ions accessing red P in the deep pore.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of red P, HHPCNSs, and the HHPCNSs/P composite. (c) XPS survey spectra of HHPCNSs and the HHPCNSs/P composite and (d) high-resolution P 2p XPS spectra of the red P HHPCNSs/P composite. (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm curves of HHPCNSs and the HHPCNSs/P composite and (f) pore size distributions of HHPCNSs and the HHPCNSs/P composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cyclic voltammetry (CV) was measured to investigate lithiation/delithiation behavior of the HHPCNSs/P composite electrode for LIBs under a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.5 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a displays that three reductive peaks at 0.26, 0.54, and 1.05 V can be seen during the first cathodic scan, corresponding to the continuous formation of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>P (<i>x</i> = 1 to 3) intermediates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The irreversible peak at 1.05 V, which disappears in the subsequent cycles, is caused by both activation of electrode materials and formation of an SEI layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> In the anodic scan, there are two peaks at 1.09 and 1.35 V for all three cycles, suggesting the continuous conversion reaction of Li<sub>3</sub>P to P. The subsequent two CV curves exhibit stable reductive/oxidation peaks, revealing the outstanding stability of the HHPCNSs/P composite. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b exhibits charge/discharge profiles of the first three cycles of the HHPCNSs/P composite at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, in which the plateaus coincide with the CV results. In the first cycle, high discharge/charge capacities of 3181.9 and 2463.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 77.4% are obtained, which are calculated based on the mass of red P. The irreversible capacity loss is due to generation of an SEI film. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c displays the rate performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite and the mixture from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the corresponding charge–discharge profiles are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>. The HHPCNSs/P composite exhibits steady reversible capacities of 2261.2, 2149.1, 1960.3, 1785.0, 1548.1, 1272.7, and 842.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, while the HHPCNSs/P mixture delivers poor capacities, especially which are negligible at 5 and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. As the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 2067.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained, suggesting the well-maintained nanostructure of the HHPCNSs/P composite after high rate discharge/charge. The outstanding rate performance can be ascribed to the hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure and the ultralarge pore volume, which enhance the electrode–electrolyte contact area and cushion the volume expansion. The cycling performances of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, and the discharge/charge curves at various cycle number are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>. The HHPCNSs/P mixture exhibits an initial discharge capacity of 2007.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, but the corresponding charge capacity drops to 637.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a low ICE of 31.7%, and it remains a poor capacity of 162.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. In sharp contrast, the HHPCNSs/P composite delivers an ultrahigh charge capacity of 2135.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which slightly decreases to 1876.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles with an ICE of 80.0%. Capacity retention is calculated to be 87.9%, and the average CE is around 99.6%. The superior cycling performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite arises from the ultralarge pore volume, which protects the nanostructure from breaking during the discharge/charge process. To further investigate the high-rate long cycling performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite, the electrode was measured at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 1000 cycles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. The HHPCNSs/P composite exhibits high first charge capacities of 1500.1 and 1239.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and capacities slightly decrease until 100 cycles. After that, the electrode retains a steady discharging and charging, which delivers capacities of 1201.6 and 938.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles with capacity retentions of 80.1% and 75.7%, respectively. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy of the HHPCNSs/P composite is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The charge-transfer resistances decrease after cycling in both electrodes, arising from the activation of the electrode and improved electrode–electrolyte wetting. Compared to the HHPCNSs/P mixture, the HHPCNSs/P composite displays smaller charge-transfer resistances owing to the carbon hierarchical pore network. The electrochemical performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite is much better than that of the HHPCNSs/P mixture, which is due to the intimate contact between P and HHPCNSs through the V–C strategy and the decreased size of red P by the hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical properties of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture for LIBs. (a) CV curves of the HHPCNSs/P composite half-cell at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Discharge/charge curves of the HHPCNSs/P composite electrode between 0.01 and 2.5 V at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate capacities measured at current densities ranging from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (d) cyclic performance at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Long-term cyclic stability of the HHPCNSs/P composite electrode for 1000 cycles at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The outstanding electrochemical performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite for LIBs encourages us to further test it for the SIBs. The electrochemical behavior of the HHPCNSs/P composite was measured by CV. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a displays a small cathodic peak appearing at ∼0.71 V in the initial cycle, corresponding to generation of an SEI film on the electrode surface. The corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles tested at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The HHPCNSs/P composite delivers high discharge and charge capacities of 3151.1 and 2367.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an ICE of 75.1%. The large capacity loss is caused by the generation of SEI layers. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c exhibits the rate capability of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture for SIBs, and the corresponding charge–discharge profiles are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>. Obviously, the HHPCNSs/P composite displays a better rate performance, which delivers charge capacities of 2240.1, 2106.1, 1973.5, 1830.2, 1636.8, 1236.8, and 831.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. As the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a capacity of 2047.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> still remains. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d compares cycling performances of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture anodes, and the discharge/charge curves at various cycle number are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>. The HHPCNSs/P composite displays an excellent cycling stability, and the charge capacity remains at 1674.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with CEs close to 100%. In comparison, the HHPCNSs/P mixture only delivers 127.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The excellent cycling performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite can be attributed to the ultralarge pore volume, which accommodates a huge volume change during the repeated discharge/charge process. Even at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the HHPCNSs/P composite still delivers charge capacities of 1269.4 and 861.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles with capacity retentions of 81.2% and 70.1% and CEs close to 100%. The excellent electrochemical property for SIBs can be attributed to honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure with ultralarge pore volume, which can not only increase electrode–electrolyte contact area and accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> traveling to improve capacity and rate property but also have a large space to withstand huge volume change of red P during cycling due to ultralarge pore volume. In addition, the honeycomb-like hierarchical porous nanostructure decreases interfacial charge-transfer impedance to improve the conductivity, which is proved by EIS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The charge-transfer resistances of the HHPCNSs/P composite anode are much lower than that of the HHPCNSs/P mixture before cycling and after 10 cycles. The decrease of the charge-transfer resistances after cycling for both LIBs and SIBs can be ascribed to the improved electrode–electrolyte wetting and the formation of a conductive SEI layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref34" aria-label="reference 25 and 34">(25,34)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture for SIBs. (a) CV curves for the first three cycles of the HHPCNSs/P composite. (b) Discharge/charge voltage profiles of the HHPCNSs/P composite at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture at different current densities. (d) Cycling performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Long cycling performance of the HHPCNSs/P composite at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further study the lithium/sodium-ion storage behavior, CV was tested at various scan rates from 0.1 to 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a and e</a>, the peak current increases and covers a higher potential range with the increasing scan rates of both LIBs and SIBs. Herein, the reaction kinetics can be investigated by the following equations: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>i</i> is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters. The <i>b</i>-value can be calculated by fitting the slope of the line in log(<i>v</i>) <i>versus</i> log(<i>i</i>) plots, and the <i>b</i>-values of anodic peaks for LIBs and SIBs are calculated to be 0.68 and 0.62, respectively, suggesting the capacitive process dominates both Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior, which leads to the outstanding rate performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b and f</a>). Furthermore, the proportion of capacitive contribution can be calculated by the following equation: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are the constants determined by the linear plots of <i>i</i>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> and ν<sup>1/2</sup> at a given potential. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c and g</a> display the pseudocapacitive contribution at a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively, where the red region takes about 84.1% and 80.7% of the total area of the CV curve. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9d and h</a> exhibit the pseudocapacitive contributions at other scan rates and the normalized percentages of pseudocapacitance, revealing the ultrafast lithiation/delithiation and sodiation/desodiation.</div><div class="NLM_p">We further investigated the practical application prospect of the HHPCNSs/P composite through the evaluation of lithium/sodium-ion full batteries with LiFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as cathode for LIBs and SIBs, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and b display the discharge/charge curves, which exhibit average charge plateaus of 2.25 and 2.75 V and discharge plateaus of 2.35 and 2.95 V for Li/Na-ion full batteries, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c and d show the excellent rate property for both Li/Na-ion full batteries, revealing a practical application of fast charging/discharging. The cycling performance of Li-ion full batteries at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 200 cycles is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. The capacity remains at 834.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles, corresponding to 92.1% capacity retention. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f shows the cycling performance of Na-ion full batteries, which remains at 793.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles, corresponding to a 90.2% capacity retention. The excellent electrochemical performances for both LIBs and SIBs are much better than the recently reported P/C composite (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical properties of the HHPCNSs/P composite for Li/Na-ion full batteries. Charge/discharge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> of (a) HHPCNSs/P composite//LiFePO<sub>4</sub> full batteries and (b) HHPCNSs/P composite//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full batteries. (c, d) Rate performance for Li/Na-ion full batteries, respectively. (e, f) Cycling performance at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for Li/Na-ion full batteries, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The outstanding electrochemical property of the HHPCNSs/P composite can be ascribed to the design of the nanostructure. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch2" aria-label="Scheme 2">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch2" aria-label="2">2</a> presents the advantages of HHPCNSs as the host of red P. The ultralarge pore volume provides the possibility of high load of red P, and the nanostructure with uniform distribution of hierarchical micro–mesopores makes the red P uniformly small and root in the micropores of HHPCNSs like “trees”, resulting in red P being well fixed. Hence the “trees” cannot fall off because of volume variation during cycling. During the discharge process, small red P “trees” grow up and change to Li<sub>3</sub>P/Na<sub>3</sub>P “big trees”, but the uniform distribution of micropores can be good for keeping the “big trees” in place through the immobilization effect of the “tree root”. On the contrary, red P “trees” do not root in nonpore carbon materials and absorb few red P on the surface, leading to an unstable cycling performance. This is because the red P easily falls down from the surface after changing to Li<sub>3</sub>P/Na<sub>3</sub>P “big trees”. In addition, the ultralarge pore volume provides sufficient space for electrolyte ions to access red P in the deep pore and accommodates a huge volume change of red P as well. Although P changes to Li<sub>3</sub>P/Na<sub>3</sub>P, the ultralarge pore volume still has some spare space, which can more effectively alleviate the expansion of the volume. Moreover, the highly conductive porous nanostructure of the HHPCNSs provides a rapid and efficient electron transport network to improve the conductivity.</div><figure id="sch2" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/medium/nn9b07428_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 2. Schematic illustration of the advantages of the HHPCNSs as the host of red P.</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/asset/images/large/nn9b07428_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.9b07428&amp;id=sch2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Furthermore, to prove the stability of the honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure, SEM images of the HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture are probed after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a and c</a>, the HHPCNSs/P composite still maintain a honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure after 100 cycles for both LIBs and SIBs, revealing an excellent structural stability of the electrode. By comparison, most of the HHPCNSs/P mixture agglomerates together, although a few nanospheres can be seen (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b and d</a>). The SEM images of the HHPCNSs/P composite electrode after 1000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a and b</a>. No obvious cracked nanospheres can be seen even at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for both LIBs and SIBs, and the morphology of the HHPCNSs/P composite is well maintained and highly dispersed, demonstrating the outstanding structural stability of the HHPCNSs/P composite at high current density.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the HHPCNSs/P composite is synthesized through a facile V–C process, which is used as anodes for LIBs and SIBs. Benefiting from hierarchical micro–mesoporous and ultralarge pore volume, the HHPCNSs/P composite exhibits an ultrahigh capacity (1876.1 and 1674.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles for LIBs and SIBs, respectively), outstanding rate property (842.2 and 831.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively), and excellent cycling stability (1201.6 and 938.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retentions of 80.1% and 75.7% after 1000 cycles for LIBs and 1269.4 and 861.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retentions of 81.2% and 70.1% after 1000 cycles for SIBs). Moreover, the Li/Na-ion full batteries exhibit high capacity and excellent rate and cycling performances, which further confirms the practicability of HHPCNSs/P composite. The outstanding electrochemical performance of both lithium/sodium-ion batteries is much better than currently reported P/C materials. This work powerfully demonstrates that the HHPCNSs/P composite is a promising anode candidate for both LIBs and SIBs, and the honeycomb-like hierarchical micro–mesoporous nanostructure with ultralarge pore volume has great guiding significance for encapsulating active materials with tremendous volume variation and controlled evaporation temperatures like sulfur.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Synthesis of the HHPCNSs/P Composite</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The details of the synthesis of PAA-Ca nanospheres and hierarchical porous carbon nanospheres can be found in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. A 40 mg amount of as-prepared HHPCNSs was hand-milled together with 60 mg of red P powder in the mortar inside an Ar-filled glovebox and then vacuum-sealed. The ampule tube was heated to 500 °C for 3 h, followed by cooling to 260 °C for 24 h. After cooling to room temperature, the HHPCNSs/P composite was collected, washed with carbon disulfide to remove the residual white P, and dried in a vacuum oven at 50 °C overnight.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="ext-link">10.1021/acsnano.9b07428</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM image of HHPCNSs/P mixture, photographs of PAA-Ca solution before and after adding IPA and the weight of HHPCNSs powder, TGA curve of HHPCNSs/P composite, high-resolution P 2p XPS spectra of red P and HHPCNSs/P mixture, corresponding charge/discharge profiles of HHPCNSs/P for LIBs/SIBs, pseudocapacitive contributions at different scan rates, EIS spectra of HHPCNSs/P composite and mixture before cycling and after 10 cycles for LIBs/SIBs, SEM images of HHPCNSs/P of the recently reported carbon materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.9b07428/suppl_file/nn9b07428_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controlled synthesis of mesoporous hollow SnO2 nanococoons with enhanced lithium storage capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Lu; Guan, Bo; Zhang, Lingyu; Su, Zhongmin; Xie, Haiming; Wang, Chungang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">22021-22025</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Mesoporous hollow SnO2 nanococoons (mh-SnO2 NCs) were synthesized via the redox reaction between SnCl2 and Fe2O3 nanospindles in the presence of excess SnCl4.  The Fe2O3 nanospindles act as a sacrificial template to oxidize SnCl2 to form a layer of SnO2 on the original Fe2O3 core surface and the following hydrolysis of SnCl4 on the as-generated SnO2 further produces SnO2 nanoparticles (NPs) to solidify the mh-SnO2 shell, resulting in the formation of a hierarchical structure on the surface.  The surface morphol. of mh-SnO2 NCs could be easily controlled by varying the synthesis parameters.  The resulting mh-SnO2 NCs possess superior lithium storage properties with an exceptionally good cycling performance and an excellent rate capability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA07272D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huo, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-Pot Controllable Synthesis of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Solid, Hollow and Multi-Shell Hollow Nanospheres as Superior Anode Materials for Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-pot controllable synthesis of CoFe2O4 solid, hollow and multi-shell hollow nanospheres as superior anode materials for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qi, Yun; Liu, Bingqiu; Zhang, Lingyu; Huo, Yuqiu; Li, Lu; Xie, Haiming; Wang, Chungang; Su, Zhongmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">41</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">21994-22003</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A straightforward and general strategy for the large-scale synthesis of hollow metal oxide shells with controlled size, compn. and internal structure is highly desirable owing to their significant applications in energy storage devices such as lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  In this work, we design for the first time a one-pot and cost-effective method to controllably fabricate CoFe2O4 solid nanospheres (CFO-SNSs), CoFe2O4 hollow nanospheres (CFO-HNSs) and multi-shell CoFe2O4 hollow nanospheres (MS-CFO-HNSs) on a large scale by just varying the calcination conditions.  Compared with CFO-SNSs and CFO-HNSs, as anode materials for LIBs, MS-CFO-HNSs deliver a superior lithium storage capacity, which stabilize at a reversible capacity as high as 1354 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles at a c.d. of 500 mA g-1.  Even at the high current densities of 1000 and 5000 mA g-1, the specific capacities of MS-CFO-HNSs are still up to 1041 and 570 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles (88.9% and 86.9% of the capacity retention), which break the previous records and exhibit the best cycling performance among the as-reported ternary transition metal oxide (TTMO) materials with the same structures or ingredients owing to the unique nanoscale size of MS-CFO-HNSs, thus providing a preview of their practical applications in LIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201200791"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Selected-Control Synthesis of Monodisperse Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C Core-Shell Spheres, Chains, and Rings as High-Performance Anode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. - Eur.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Colloidal Synthesis of Silicon-Carbon Composite Material for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Haiping; Barragan, Alejandro A.; Geng, Linxiao; Long, Donghui; Ling, Licheng; Bozhilov, Krassimir N.; Mangolini, Lorenzo; Guo, Juchen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">56</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">36</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10780-10785</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The colloidal routes are reported to synthesize silicon@carbon composites for the first time.  Surface-functionalized Si nanoparticles (SiNPs) dissolved in styrene and hexadecane are used as the dispersed phase in oil-in-water emulsions, from which yolk-shell and dual-shell hollow SiNPs@C composites are produced via polymn. and subsequent carbonization.  As anode materials for Li-ion batteries, the SiNPs@C composites demonstrate excellent cycling stability and rate performance, which is ascribed to the uniform distribution of SiNPs within the carbon hosts.  The Li-ion anodes composed of 46 wt.% of dual-shell SiNPs@C, 46 wt.% of graphite, 5 wt.% of acetylene black, and 3 wt.% of CM-cellulose with an areal loading higher than 3 mg cm-2 achieve an overall specific capacity higher than 600 mAh g-1, which is an improvement of more than 100 % compared to the pure graphite anode.  These new colloidal routes present a promising general method to produce viable Si-C composites for Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502100"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qu, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Density Monolith of <i>N-</i>Doped Holey Graphene for Ultrahigh Volumetric Capacity of Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Biodegradable Polydopamine-Derived Electrode Material for High-Capacity and Long-Life Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Tao; Li, Zong-jun; Wang, Heng-guo; Bao, Di; Meng, Fan-lu; Zhang, Xin-bo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Polydopamine (PDA), which is biodegradable and is derived from naturally occurring products, can be employed as an electrode material, wherein controllable partial oxidization plays a key role in balancing the proportion of redox-active carbonyl groups and the structural stability and cond. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2540</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2548</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C7TA07683E</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D2540-2548%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DD.%2BZhao%26author%3DJ.%2BZhang%26author%3DC.%2BFu%26author%3DJ.%2BHuang%26author%3DD.%2BXiao%26author%3DM.%2BM.%2BF.%2BYuen%26author%3DC.%2BNiu%26title%3DEnhanced%2BCycling%2BStability%2Bof%2BRing-Shaped%2BPhosphorus%2Binside%2BMulti-Walled%2BCarbon%2BNanotubes%2Bas%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC7TA07683E&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.9b07428&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC7TA07683E&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced cycling stability of ring-shaped phosphorus inside multi-walled carbon nanotubes as anodes for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Dan; Zhang, Jinying; Fu, Chengcheng; Huang, Jialiang; Xiao, Dingbin; Yuen, Matthew M. F.; Niu, Chunming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2540-2548</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A ring-shaped phosphorus allotrope has recently been synthesized inside multi-walled carbon nanotubes (r-P@MWCNTs).  The electrochem. properties of the r-P@MWCNTs as anodes for lithium-ion batteries were investigated.  External phosphorus was found to degrade rapidly during charging and discharging cycles.  The internal ring-shaped phosphorus is well stabilized by the surrounding carbon nanotube walls to provide an extremely high cycling stability.  The specific capacities of r-P@MWCNTs degrade in the first dozens of cycles and then tend to be stable.  The insertion and extn. of lithium ions through MWCNTs to the encapsulated phosphorus are believed to occur through the defects and interlayer gaps of MWCNTs.  The vol. expansion of phosphorus during lithiation is eased along the axis of MWCNTs since the MWCNTs are not fully filled by phosphorus; this prevents the encapsulated phosphorus from escaping and further resulting in capacity degrdn.  A specific capacity of 444 mA h g-1 based on the whole composites, contg. ∼16% phosphorus, is still maintained after 500 cycles at a current rate of 500 mA g-1.  The electrochem. performances of ring-shaped phosphorus inside MWCNTs were demonstrated to be much higher than those of different phosphorus phases grown on the outside surfaces of MWCNTs under same conditions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b04957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boosting Potassium-Ion Battery Performance by Encapsulating Red Phosphorus in Free-Standing Nitrogen-Doped Porous Hollow Carbon Nanofibers</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>19</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1351</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1358</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b04957</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D19%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D1351-1358%26journal%3DNano%2BLett.%26author%3DY.%2BWu%26author%3DS.%2BHu%26author%3DR.%2BXu%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.%2BPeng%26author%3DQ.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.%2BYu%26title%3DBoosting%2BPotassium-Ion%2BBattery%2BPerformance%2Bby%2BEncapsulating%2BRed%2BPhosphorus%2Bin%2BFree-Standing%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BPorous%2BHollow%2BCarbon%2BNanofibers%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.nanolett.8b04957&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.9b07428&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.8b04957&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boosting Potassium-Ion Battery Performance by Encapsulating Red Phosphorus in Free-Standing Nitrogen-Doped Porous Hollow Carbon Nanofibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Ying; Hu, Shuhe; Xu, Rui; Wang, Jiawei; Peng, Zhangquan; Zhang, Qiaobao; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1351-1358</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) are a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage owning to the natural abundance and low cost of potassium.  However, the biggest challenge for KIBs application lies in the lack of suitable electrode materials that can deliver long cycle life and high reversible capacity.  In this work, we realized unprecedented long cycle life with high reversible capacity (465 mAh g-1 at 2 A g-1 after 800 cycles) as well as outstanding rate capability (342 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1) for KIBs by embedding red P into free-standing nitrogen-doped porous hollow carbon nanofibers (red P@N-PHCNFs).  This design circumvents the problems of pulverization and aggregation of P particles.  The in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) investigation reveals the structural robustness of the composite fibers during potassiation.  The formation of P-C chem. bonds as well as nitrogen doping in the carbon matrix can facilitate the sturdy contact and enhance the adsorption energy of P atoms evidenced by DFT results.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Well-dispersed phosphorus nanocrystals within carbon via high-energy mechanical milling for high performance lithium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xianyang; Chen, Gen; Le, Zaiyuan; Li, Xinru; Nie, Ping; Liu, Xiaoyan; Xu, Pengcheng; Wu, Hao Bin; Liu, Zhuang; Lu, Yunfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its abundance and high theor. capacity, phosphorus has attracted intense research interest as anode material for lithium-ion batteries.  However, the adaptation of phosphorus for batteries is still limited by its poor electrochem. performance, which is assocd. with its poor electronic cond. and large vol. change during charging and discharging.  We herein report a facile and cost-effective method, which is based on high-energy mech. milling, for the synthesis of phosphorus nanoparticles within carbon matrix as high-performance anode materials.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultrafine red P nanoconfined between expanded graphene sheets for high-performance lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ruan, Jiafeng; Pang, Yuepeng; Luo, Sainan; Yuan, Tao; Peng, Chengxin; Yang, Junhe; Zheng, Shiyou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

It is found that an appropriate interlaminar distance of graphene sheets, which is achieved by tailoring the mass ratio of graphene and carbon nanotubes, can ensure high loading content and effective nanoconfinement of red P simultaneously, resulting in high electrochem. performances for the P@expanded-G samples.  For instance, the optimized P@expanded-G50 anode exhibits a specific capacity as high as 1010 mA h g-1 based on the total mass (2053 mA h g-1 based on the red P mass) with a coulombic efficiency close to 100% after 500 cycles at 0.1 A g-1.  Moreover, as the c.d. increases to 2.0 A g-1, the specific capacity still remains over 440 mA h g-1 (based on the total mass) with progressive cycling up to more than 2000 cycles.  Structural characterization reveals that the existence of a large amt. of uniformly nanoconfined red P between the expanded graphene sheets and the formation of P-C and P-O bonds are mainly responsible for the much improved electrochem. performances.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201802140"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Red Phosphorus Nanoparticle@3D Interconnected Carbon Nanosheet Framework Composite for Potassium-Ion Battery Anodes</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1802140</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoconfinement of red phosphorus nanoparticles in seaweed-derived hierarchical porous carbonaceous fibers for enhanced lithium ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Tao; Li, Daohao; Chen, Shuai; Sun, Yuanyuan; Zhang, Huawei; Xia, Yanzhi; Yang, Dongjiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
